# Complete Guide to Python for Data Engineering: From Beginner to Advanced

> Get up and running with the basics of Python before progressing to more advanced topics specific to data engineering. In this hands-on, interactive course, join instructor Deepak Goyal to practice performing a wide range of data engineering tasks in Python to boost your technical know-how, prepare for an interview, or land a new role. This course includes Code Challenges powered by CoderPad. Code 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced) | 5h 28m | Advanced | 5K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=15)** The secret lies in the [[Data Engineering]] and the magic wand that makes it possible is [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]].
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=23)** Welcome to the Python for Data Engineering.

> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=27)** Let's start with the basic.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=29)** What is data engineering?
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=31)** In simple terms, it's a process of collecting, validating, storing, and processing data to turn it into information that can be used to make decisions.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=45)** Think of it like a factory.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=47)** Our raw materials, or in our case, our raw data, comes in, they are processed and transformed, and out come a finished product or a useful information.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=62)** Now, question comes, where does Pythons comes into the play?
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=67)** Python is a programming language known for its simplicity and versatility.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=75)** It's like a Swiss Army knife of programming language.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=80)** It's used in [[Web Development]], [[Game Development]], and yes, in data engineering too.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=87)** Python is particularly popular in data engineering because of its powerful libraries, like [[Pandas (Software)|pandas]] for data manipulations, NumPy for numerical computation, and Matplotlib for data visualization.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=103)** Let's take a real-world example.

> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-python-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=147)** By the end, you will have the skills you need to harness the power of data and make informed decisions.

#### [Setting up your Python environment](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/setting-up-your-python-environment?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/setting-up-your-python-environment?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Imagine having a powerful computer that you can access from anywhere, anytime, without worrying about installation, configuration, or even the cost.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/setting-up-your-python-environment?u=76281980&t=13)** Sounds like a dream, right?
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/setting-up-your-python-environment?u=76281980&t=15)** Well, with [[Google]] Colab, this dream becomes a reality.
>

> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=216)** for i in range(5): Now, in this case, what it's going to do is, it will keep on printing the value of this i, until unless we reach to a range of five.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=238)** So, for i in the range, it will start the value of i, and every time, it will increment that value by one, and it will go until unless it reaches to five.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=250)** So here the idea is, this for statement will run five times, and whatever you have written under that block is going to get execute that many number of times.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=267)** Great. That's how you can use the for loop.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=270)** Now let's talk about the third type of control statement, that is while loop.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=276)** While loop is used to repeat a block of code as long as the condition is true.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=282)** It is pretty similar to the for loop, only the change is the way we write this loop.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=289)** For example, let's say if I rewrite that example, it could be number =5.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=298)** And instead of using either a for, I'm using the while.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=304)** And in the while, you can put the condition, for example, number &gt;0.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/control-structures?u=76281980&t=311)** Now, this will go on repetitively until unless this number is greater than zero.

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Imagine you are driving a car.

>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=39)** Python provides tools to handle error gracefully, allowing your program to recover from the errors or even prevent them from happening in the first place.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=54)** Let's start with an example in our [[Google]] Colab.

>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=74)** But what if you don't want your program to crash when it encounters an error, that's where the error handling comes in.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=85)** In Python, we can do error handling using the try accept block.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=92)** The try block contains the code that might cause an error, and except block contains the code that will be executed if error occurs.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=103)** Let's modify our code to handle zero division error.
>

>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=125)** And if this error occurs, what I want to do is print("Sorry you are dividing by Zero").
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=137)** Now let me try to run this now this time it runs very gracefully.

> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=142)** We try to divide by zero, but instead of giving an exception, it is giving me, sorry you are dividing by zero.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=150)** This happened because we have handled the expected error in a very graceful manner.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=158)** In some cases, you might not very sure what specific error can occur.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=165)** In those case, you can, instead of typing an error type, you just type except and let's run the code.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=176)** This time it tried to handle any kind of error which occurs in this piece of a code.

> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=183)** That's how you can cut a general error in Python.

> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=189)** Finally, we can use the else and finally blocks to add more control to our error handling.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=196)** The else block contains the code that will be executed if no errors occurs.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=204)** For example, let me retype this code and here after this except I put a code, something like else block and there I'm saying, print("Nothing wrong happened").

> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=225)** We can also have an another block that is finally that will execute no matter what happened.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=233)** Whether you got an error, or whether you don't got an error, this will execute always.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=242)** Now, if you run this code, let's say instead of an error, I try to run this with a right number and execute.

> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=252)** This time you'll see that this got executed.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=256)** This doesn't got execute because there is no error.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=261)** Else block get execute because we say that whenever no error occurs in our try block, this will get execute, and that's why it prints, nothing wrong happened.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=271)** And finally block is always execute whether you got an error, or whether you don't got an error.

> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/error-handling?u=76281980&t=279)** So that's how this output get come.

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Assume that you are a librarian and you have a vast collection of books.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=6)** You need to organize them by genre, author, and title so that readers can find what they are looking for.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=15)** In [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]], collections are like this library, and with the right tools, you can organize and manipulate your data efficiently.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=27)** In Python, a collection is a container that holds multiple items.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=33)** These items can be of any type, and a single collection can hold items of different types.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=42)** Python provides several types of collections.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=46)** List, a dynamic arrays that can grow or shrink in size.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=52)** Imagine them as flexible containers where you can keep adding or removing items.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=60)** Tuples, immutable sequences, often used to represent records or structures.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=67)** Think of them as a fixed set of data that don't change, like the specification of a machine.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=75)** Sets, an order collection of unique items.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=80)** They are like a bag where you throw items.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=83)** And even if you throw in duplicates, the bag only keeps one of each.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=90)** Dictionaries, key value pairs perfect for structured data.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=96)** Imagine a massive library where each book or a value can be quickly found by its unique title that is key.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=107)** In [[Data Engineering]], choosing the right collection can make your operation faster, more efficient, and more intuitive.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=115)** Remember, while each collection has its strengths, its combination of them that gives Python its power in [[Data Manipulation]] and engineering.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/collection-overview?u=76281980&t=128)** That's wrap up of our broad overview of Python collection.

>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=8)** But once you have set the coordinates, they can't be changed.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=14)** This is pretty similar to tuples in the [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]].

> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=29)** It means that they are immutable.

> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=37)** You can access them via index as well.

>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=78)** Remember, tuple creation can be done while defining under the brackets.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=85)** Also, one thing you notice that this tuple, elements are heterogeneous, like first element is my integer, second a string, then another string, and then integer.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=98)** So it is not mandatory that all of the tuple elements are of the same type.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=103)** They could be of heterogeneous type.

> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=106)** In fact, even if you want to access any of the specific element, you can access them via index.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=115)** So you can give the index something like one.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=119)** So this will going to print me the name that is Deepak.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=123)** Remember that the index are zero indexed.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=128)** It means that the first position would be zero, second position would be one.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=133)** So if you want to get this second element from the tuple, you will use the index one.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=141)** There is another trick.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/python-collections-tuples?u=76281980&t=142)** Imagine you have to print the last element of this tuple and you don't know the size of this tuple.
>

> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=324)** The second element, also false.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=326)** The third element is false.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=328)** But the fourth element is true, and fifth element is also true.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=333)** So, this kind of a boolean operation, or the logical operations, you can do on your array, element-by-element.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=343)** Besides this, NumPy also allows you another interesting feature, that is broadcasting.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=350)** For example, if you want to apply some operation on your array, then you can do that in this manner.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=359)** For example, I would say that array1 +5.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=364)** Now what I'm trying to do here is, I am adding this scalar value of 5 to all the elements of an array.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=372)** So I would say that I am broadcasting this element, 5, to all the elements of an array.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=379)** And if I just go and try to see the result, you will find that this is scalar value to all the elements.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=388)** This is a little different from addition of two arrays.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=392)** Earlier, we have seen addition of two arrays, where we are adding, element by element.
>
> **[6:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=398)** But here, we are broadcasting the single value to all the element, and that is called as the broadcasting in NumPy.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/array-operations?u=76281980&t=409)** So, we covered the couple of methods, like arithmetic, logical operations, and this kind of a broadcasting in this video.

#### [Understanding classes and objects](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Ever wondered how applications like [[Banking]], online warehouse management, social media platforms are built?
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=9)** It all starts with classes and object.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=12)** Think of classes as a blueprint for creating objects with certain attributes and behaviors.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=20)** For example, if we consider car as a class, then its state or attribute would be color, weight, model name, and its behavior would be run, reverse, apply brakes.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=36)** Objects are considered to be an instance of the class.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=40)** For example, car represents the class, while a specific car, like BMW, Mercedes, with specific name, color, and model number would be an object.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=54)** In real world, most of the complex software system build using the [[Object-Oriented Programming (OOP)|object-oriented programming]] design principles.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=64)** That is, the breaking of things in terms of classes and object.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=68)** Another good example would be banking application, where account could be the class and an individual account for [[John the Ripper|John]] could be an object.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=80)** Classes are reusable in nature.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=83)** You define it once and can be used many time.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=87)** Now think about the objects that are currently surrounding you as you watch the video.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=93)** Make a mental list of their attributes and their behaviors.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-classes-obejct?u=76281980&t=98)** Think about this for a moment, and then go to the next video where you learn how you can implement all of these in practical manner.

> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/tips-to-write-efficent-python-code?u=76281980&t=50)** Remember, function is used to do the reusable piece of a code, but if you call this functions within the loops, then every time, this will get called, and it might be doing a very small piece of work internally.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/tips-to-write-efficent-python-code?u=76281980&t=69)** Sometimes over-calling the functions many times can slow down the process.

>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/tips-to-write-efficent-python-code?u=76281980&t=85)** Same thing happens into the Python world as well.

>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/tips-to-write-efficent-python-code?u=76281980&t=113)** Tip number three.

>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=72)** It can distribute the task across multiple machines, which makes it incredibly fast, and it also allows you to scale the solution.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=87)** If today you have a five node cluster, you can increase it to any number.
>

>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=105)** This speed is even faster than the speed which you get into the [[Hadoop]].

>

>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=126)** Whenever a work or your job get comes in, it'll divide the work into task, all those task runs on the worker nodes within the PySpark cluster, and all these runs in parallel to give you the speed, and gives you the distributed computing environment.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=150)** Eventually, this helps to manage and process a huge amount of data, even with a very small processing time.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=162)** Another benefit is real time processing.
>

>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=173)** The moment somebody is trying to make a transaction and you want to catch him, that moment itself.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=180)** So probably that kind of real time streaming solution is needed, and PySpark fortunately can provide you that functionality as well.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=192)** It also benefits in streamlining the ETL processes.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=197)** ETL process are fundamental in [[Data Engineering]].
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=202)** PySpark streamlined this process by handling the extraction, transformation, and loading of a data at scale, reducing the time and efforts required.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=216)** In natural, PySpark empowers data engineer to tackle the challenges posed by big data, real time processing, and complex computations.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understand-pyspark-for-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=229)** It's like having an high performance engine under the hood, which enable the [[Data-driven Decision Making]] on a massive scale.

#### [What is Hadoop](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Think about social media platforms like [[Facebook]], Twitter, or [[Instagram]].
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=7)** They have billions of users, generating an enormous amount of data every second.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=14)** And let's say, if I ask you to analyze that trend by reading all that with, probably you don't have anything which can do that.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=25)** The answer for such kind of an analysis is the [[Hadoop]].
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=29)** Hadoop is the framework which can give you the superpower to analyze even and [[Unstructured Data]], and that too in the form of a very huge data, which we call [[Big Data]].
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=43)** Hadoop is an engine for processing and analyzing the massive amount of a data.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=50)** You might be wondering how they get this power in the Hadoop.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=55)** The reason is that it follows the distributed computing or the parallel processing.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=63)** So it has a cluster.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=66)** So when you work in the Hadoop, you have to have a cluster of a multiple nodes, and your work get divided in these number of nodes, which works in parallel for analyzing this data.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=80)** It also implement default tolerance because the moment you go to the distributed computing, there is chances that a few of the machine might go down and then your entire work can get disrupted.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=94)** But Hadoop handles that situation by using the fault tolerance mechanism, where it'll handle any abrupt failure of any of the node.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=106)** It also have the [[Scalability]] feature on.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=110)** That means that, today, if you have a 10-node cluster and tomorrow your data size grows like anything, you can easily add 10 machines more and you can double the computation power.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=124)** So it has a very horizontal scalability option.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=128)** And lastly, when you have the horizontal scalability option there, eventually it allows him to manage a huge amount of a [[Data Storage]] in an efficient manner as well.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=143)** So overall, Hadoop is the framework which helps you to store a huge amount of data and that can help you to analyze such a massive data with very good performance.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-hadoop?u=76281980&t=158)** In the next video, we'll see an another technology that is the Spark, which is even far better than the Hadoop.

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/onprem-vs-cloud-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=9)** This means owning and maintaining servers, storage, and networking equipments on your own.

> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=24)** HTML stands for HyperText Markup Language.

>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=47)** Here it's a simple example of HTML.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=51)** You can look at it.

>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=68)** Your HTML tag is a root element and it has multiple nested elements like head, title, body, h1, and others.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=82)** HTML is made up of tags and elements.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=86)** Tags are the building blocks that define elements.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=90)** Most tags comes in pairs and opening tag, and there is corresponding closing tag for them.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=99)** For example, you can see that h1 and correspondingly /h1.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=106)** So, first one is an opening tag.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=108)** The second one is the closing tag.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=111)** And whatever you put in between the opening tag and the closing tag will be a content between these two tag.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=120)** Similarly, we can have a paragraph p tag and /p.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=126)** That is the closing tag for this paragraph tag.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=131)** Some tags are self-closing, such as the tag for images.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=136)** In cases when you are defining some kind of an image onto your webpages, in that case, you can just define the tag img, which has not any closing tag.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=149)** Similarly, we have another tag for breaking the line.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=153)** That is br tag.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=155)** That also does not have any closing tag.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=159)** Tags can also have attributes that provide additional information about the elements.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=166)** Attributes are always included in the opening tag and comes in name/value pairs.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=173)** For example, if I show you here, you can see that in this example we have an href is an attribute for the anchor tag.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=184)** Anchor tag starts with a, and it has a attribute href, which is representing a specific URL, and then Visit [Example.com](https://Example.com).
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=196)** The text will become a hyperlink.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=199)** It happens because we have added in anchor tag a.

> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-basics?u=76281980&t=215)** An HTML document typically includes several key sections.

#### [HTML parents, children, and descendants](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Understanding the relationships between the [[HTML]] tags, the parents, child, and descendant is crucial for effectively navigating and manipulating the HTML content.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=15)** Let's dive in and decode the hierarchical structure of HTML.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=21)** HTML documents are structured hierarchically.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=25)** Similar to a family tree, the hierarchy is composed of elements that have parents, child, and descendant relationship.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=36)** Visualizing HTML in this manner helps us in navigating and manipulating the document more efficiently.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=46)** In HTML, a parent element is a one that contains other elements within it.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=53)** For example, if you look at the following HTML code snippet, you will find that there is a &lt;body&gt; tag, and within the &lt;body&gt; tag, you can see the &lt;h1&gt; tag, you can see the parent &lt;p&gt; tag.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=68)** So in this case, you can say that that &lt;body&gt; tag is a parent tag, and my &lt;h1&gt; tag and my &lt;p&gt; tag here, that is a paragraph tag, is considered to be a children tag.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=83)** So basically, the child elements are the direct descendant of a parent element.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=90)** They are nested within the parent tags.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=93)** For example here, the paragraph, &lt;p&gt; tag, is within the body directly.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=100)** It's important to note that the children are only those elements which are directly nested within a parent, not deeper nested elements.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=112)** Descendants refer to any element nested within a parent element, including children, grandchildren, and great-grandchildren and so on.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/html-parents-children-and-descendants?u=76281980&t=125)** In the following HTML snippet, if you look at it, you can find that the &lt;span&gt; tag is not directly within the &lt;body&gt; tag, but it is a little deep nested.

#### [BeautifulSoup basics](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now it's time to get into an understanding of the tools which can help you to retrieve web scraping, and we are focusing on a library that is Beautiful Soup.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=11)** Beautiful Soup is a powerful library for parsing [[HTML]] and XML document, allowing you to navigate and manipulate the web content in an easiest manner.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=25)** Let's get started.

>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=37)** If you remember, we learned that in the HTML webpages, we have a kind of a relationship there.

>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=76)** The library handles the intricacies of web scraping by allowing you to extract the data easily, even from a very poorly formatted HTML.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=90)** This makes it an invaluable tool for [[Data Engineering]] and working with the web data.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=97)** Beautiful Soup works by parsing HTML into a tree of [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]] objects, such as tags, navigablestrings, or the comments.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=109)** These objects can be queried and manipulated.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=113)** The process involves the multiple steps.

>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=121)** Now, Beautiful Soup takes in the raw HTML and convert it into the structured tree format.
>

> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=266)** How you will do it?

> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=267)** First, you will identify the structure of the blog HTML.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=272)** You will use Beautiful Soup to find the element containing the blog post data.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=278)** Then, you extract the required data fields and store them in a structured format.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=285)** This approach allows you to automate the [[Data Collection]] from multiple blog posts very efficiently.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=293)** Once you have the parsed HTML document, navigating the parse tree is crucial for extracting the data.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=302)** Beautiful Soup provides several ways to travel the tree.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=307)** Tag navigation, this is used to access the child tags by their tag names.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=313)** Attributes, you can access the tag attributes directly.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=318)** Navigablestrings, extract the text content with the tags.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=324)** Parent/child relationship traverses up and down the tree using the parent and child relationship.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=332)** Understanding this navigation technique is essential for effective data exploration.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=339)** Challenges in using the Beautiful Soup.

> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/beautifulsoup-basics?u=76281980&t=348)** For example, handling the poorly formatted HTML.

#### [filter() and map() functions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are two more powerful functions in the [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]] for data engineers.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=7)** The filter and the map.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=9)** These functions are essential for transforming and filtering the data, making your [[Data Processing]] workflows more efficient and concise.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=21)** Let's explore how they work and where you can use them.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=27)** The filter and the map functions are built-in Python functions that allow you to transform and filter the data in a [[Functional Programming]] style.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=39)** The filter function takes a function and an iterable as an argument.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=46)** It returns an iterator and then those items of the iterable.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=52)** In short filter will help you to filter out the items from your iterables.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=60)** The map function applies a given function to all the items in the iterable.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filter-map-functions?u=76281980&t=67)** For example, imagine you have a list of numbers and if you want to double all this number values individually, you can think of using the map function and that map function, you can pass this iterable list and pass your function, which is like doubling the values, correct?

#### [Logging best practices](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Proper logging is important for debugging, monitoring, and maintaining your applications.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=7)** By following the best practices, you can ensure your logging strategy is efficient, effective, and scalable.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=17)** Let's dive in and explore how to optimize your logging practice.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=23)** Use appropriate logging levels.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=25)** Using the correct logging levels, it helps to categorize and prioritize the log messages.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=33)** Here's a quick overview of the levels and when to use them.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=39)** For example, debug.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=41)** It should be used for detailed information, which is needed to diagnose the problems.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=49)** This level is important for development and the debugging purpose.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=54)** Info, this is general information about the application's operation.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=60)** Use this level for events that are part of the normal workflow.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=65)** Warning, an indication of a potential problem or an unexpected event, use this level for recovery issues.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=75)** Error, a serious issue that has been occurred.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=80)** Use this level for the errors that need immediate attention.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=85)** Critical, a very serious error indicating a major failure.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=90)** Use this level for severe problems that may cause the application to terminate.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=97)** For example, in a payment processing system, use info to log successful transaction, warning for retriable errors, and error for failed transaction that need to be investigate.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=112)** Another important best practice you need to keep in mind is avoid logging sensitive information.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=119)** Never log sensitive information like your passwords, credit card numbers, personal data.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=126)** Because logging sensitive data can lead to the security concerns and compliance issues.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=134)** Use consistent formatting.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=136)** Consistent log formatting make it easier to read and analyze log.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=141)** Use for matters to include important information like timestamp, log levels, and message content.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=151)** Log rotations help manage log file size and prevent disc space issues.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=158)** So you should keep on rotating your logs.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=162)** Leveraging the logging configuration file.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=165)** It totally makes sense to have the configuration file separate for setting the logging properties.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=173)** That keeps your code clean, and it is easier to manage complex logging setups.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=180)** Centralized logging.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=182)** Centralized logs from different parts of your application or from multiple application can help in monitoring and debugging.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=191)** Use centralized log solutions like ELK, that stands for Elastic Search Log Stash Kibana, or Splunk.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=202)** You can also think of setting up the monitoring and alerting on specific log events or patents.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=210)** This ensures you are notified of critical issues in real time.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=216)** In a production environment, setting up alerts for error and critical log levels can ensure prompt response to the severe issues.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=228)** Another best practice you should follow is test your login configurations.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=233)** You should regularly test to ensure it works as expected.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=238)** This includes verifying log rotation, format, and integrity of log data.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=244)** You can also document your logging strategy and configuration to ensure consistency and ease of maintenance.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=254)** Great, we have explored the logging best practices in the detail.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-python-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/logging-best-practices?u=76281980&t=260)** If you follow these practices, this will make your life as a developer very easy, and this will also help your code to have the clean, scalable, and better solution.
